B004SC7B6M|4.0|Man, this was an incredibly hard review for me to write. In fact, I've been listening to The Unforgiving album for over a month now (maybe closer to a month and a half) and it's still tough for me to actually sit down and write this review. Even now, after probably 30 plus album listens, I'm still conflicted. This isn't the Within Temptation of previous albums, this is someone new, different, fresh and more mainstream. This isn't the WT I fell in love with, but could it be a completely new musical romance?     Let me back up a little bit, at least for context....    I've been a fan of WT ever since middle school when I accidentally came across \\"Ice Queen\\" online. At this point, Enter and Mother Earth were the band's only albums. I spent nearly all of middle and high school with songs from these albums on repeat, and added The Silent Force and later The Heart of Everything to the mix in college. During that time I fell in love with other symphonic metal bands (yes, WT was my first) and really grew into that epic, grand sound where metal meets classical. I know that WT's sound has evolved from album to album since they appeared on the music scene, starting as more of a doom metal band on Enter to a more symphonic metal sound on Mother Earth and The Silent Force to a symphonic rock style on The Heart of Everything.    When I started hearing about The Unforgiving album, I started to wonder. They were talking up a \\"new sound\\" (which can always be alarming), also well as a unique multi-medium concept combining music, film and comics. I was especially concerned when I heard about the comics part -I was worried that the music would be super-cheesy. I was wrong, instead WT delivered a rock album with strong 80s pop influences that is the most unexpected and diverse album from the band to date, ranging from harder rock/metal to catchy, pop-friendly rock tunes, melodic rock and even pop/dance tunes (yeah, you heard me).    My favorite song on the album is definitely \\"Iron.\\" Not only is it the closest to WT's previous sound, but it's a powerful, gut-wrenching explosion of symphonic metal with inspirational lyrics that's easy to get behind. I also found that \\"A Demon's Fate\\" was also a great addition -it's a heavier song with edgy, emotional lyrics and fabulous string sections. Then there's \\"Murder,\\" a darker, gritter song that has power, strength and takes the album into dark places.    Then there are the songs that had to grow on me due to their popp-ier nature, such as \\"Where is the Edge?,\\"  \\"Shot in the Dark\\" and \\"Faster.\\" I actually find it interesting that the album's first single was \\"Faster,\\" as it's one of the weaker songs on the album -it was probably chosen due to its catchy, and more radio-friendly sound, not the mention the overt 80s pop influences.    Then...there's \\"Sinead,\\" the song that shocked me -and probably many other WT fans. \\"Sinead\\" is a dance/pop song with strong string sections (probably the only thing that keeps me from deleting the song from my iTunes) worthy of Lady Gaga. I know, I just mentioned WT song and Gaga in the same sentence. Yeah, I can see this one becoming big in clubs. As you may expect, when I first heard this song, I freaked. Did something accidentally get put on the WT album? You have to be kidding me. What is this??    For some odd reason the slow songs didn't really do it for me on here -which is absolutely not normal for WT! Out of \\"Fire and Ice,\\" \\"Stairway to the Skies\\" and \\"Lost,\\" I'd have to go with \\"Lost\\" as the most moving (and haunting) with \\"Stairway to the Skies\\" in second.    Note on bonus tracks: the North American version is actually the most complete with \\"Empty Eyes,\\" \\"I Don't Wanna\\" and \\"The Last Dance\\" (not to be confused with \\"The Dance\\" or \\"The Last Time\\"). Other versions include \\"Empty Eyes\\" and \\"I Don't Wanna\\" along with the previously released \\"Utopia.\\"    Interestingly enough, I thought that The Unforgiving offered some of the best and most cutting-edge music of WT's career. In particular, the guitar work here is, without a doubt, the more complex and impressive guitar work the band has ever delivered. Really, almost every song has a guitar solo and it never ceases to amaze. Oh, and Sharon sound just as amazing as ever. But, some of the pop/lighter sounds here still make me cringe when I heard them...but that doesn't stop me from listening.    I know this isn't the WT I first loved, but they have found a new sound here that feels like a natural progression from their previous albums. While yes, there are something things that I still find myself cringing at, I enjoy the album for the most part. I really respect the band for doing something so diverse and different, even though it may have angered some of their fan base. The Unforgiving is an excellent album that's filled with power, inspiration and amazing creativity.

The two lead singers were presumably chosen for their youth and good looks rather than vocal ability because though Stratas and Carreras make a very credible pair of lovers their singing just isn't in the same league as the many others who have interpreted these roles - Carreras is not Pavarotti and Stratas can't hold a candle (if you'll pardon the pun!) to Freni or Fowles. Renata Scotto is a lively but rather screechy Musetta.  If the singing is not wonderful, though, it is always acceptable and the acting is excellent throughout.  Carreras is particularly suited to the role of tender and sensitive (if somewhat weak) lover (remember his Don Jose?) and he makes an irresistable Rodolfo.  Stratas looks a bit frog-faced and even for a consumptive her make-up is startlingly pale, but she has the acting ability to give us a Mimi who is youthful, vulnerable and by turns playful and pathetic. At times this is a bit overdone but she does have such big, expressive eyes!  The death scene is particularly well done. Jones, of the studio's \\"Booker T. and the MGs,\\" co-wrote the many-times covered title song, as well as \\"The Hunter.\\"  King was, of course, a greatly talented guitarist, but the studio gave him Steve Cropper as backing guitar.  Add Jones, and Isaac Hayes, as pianists; Donald (\\"Duck\\") Dunn on bass; Al Jackson Jr. on drums.  And don't forget the wailing Memphis Horns: Wayne Jackson, Andrew Love, and Joe Arnold.  Finally, I can't find it anywhere in print, but I believe I read/saw somewhere that the great stride pianist Champion Joe Dupree, who provided the same service on the Glasgow-born blues shouter Maggie Bell's magnificent take of the same song, provided the piano on King's \\"As the Years Go Passing By.\\"<br /><br />The title song here, King's signature tune, lives on, and has been covered by such talent as Eric Clapton, and Cream.  \\"I Almost Lost My Mind,\\" by Ivory Joe Hunter, and \\"The Very Thought of You,\\" by Ray Noble, show King taking a quick trip through mainstream music.  \\"Kansas City\\" was a Leiber-Stoller ditty.  \\"Crosscut Saw\\" is a masterpiece of concision.  And for funky blues, try \\"Personal Manager,\\" which King himself had a hand in writing, or \\"Down Don't Bother Me,\\" another one King wrote, on which his powerful attack and purity are well displayed.  Or \\"Laundromat Blues,\\" which was King's first hit single for Stax, in which we meet a musician who can be full of sass.  And the moody \\"As the Years Go Passing By,\\" by Deadric Malone, one of my all-time favorite songs, though I must admit I love it slightly better in Maggie Bell's femme-oriented version that was recorded on Atlantic in[[ASIN:B000BW5SO0 Queen of the Night]].<br /><br />King was born in Indianola, Mississippi, on April 25, 1923, as, coincidentally, was B.B. King - no relation, nor is the third blues King, Freddie King. However, Albert did, perhaps, like to fudge things a little: he called his favorite Gibson Flying V guitar Lucy, as B.B. King calls his guitar Lucille. I actually did get to listen to Albert live, quite a few times; his power could energize a room.  In fact, I followed Albert around for two weeks observing him on his first, possibly his only, tour of the United Kingdom, in, I believe, 1969.  I almost didn't get to go.  I was, initially, to be traveling on a ticket meant for a Memphis Horn.  But when the impresario of the tour discovered the Horns weren't coming, he was furious, and cashed in those tickets.  However, I'd already pre-sold my article on Albert to \\"Cavalier,\\" a magazine current at the time.  So Stax bought me a ticket.<br /><br />At any rate, B.B. is still with us, and still making music, but Albert, who based himself in East St. Louis, passed of a heart attack, as a relatively young man, on December 21, 1992.  He could bend a guitar to his will in ways we're not likely to see again.  A must.ippi, on April 25, 1923, as, coincidentally, was B.B. King - no relation, nor is the third blues King, Freddie King. B002RSQGQK|5.0|Alfred Hitchcock was in near-perfect form when he made \\"Rear Window,\\" a stylish, minimalistic blend of mystery and dark comedy. This thriller explores \\"what you shouldn't see\\" skilfully, with a few funny bits thrown in. And having a cast that includes Grace Kelly and James Stewart doesn't hurt either.<br /><br />Photographer L.B. \\"Jeff\\" Jeffries (Stewart) got run over during a shoot, and is crankily waiting for his cast to come off. While he does so, he spies on his neighbors -- some sleep on balconies, some argue, some weep alone, and some (\\"Miss Torso\\") dance in spandex. To make things worse, Jeff is having intimacy problems with his wealthy girlfriend Lisa (Kelly), because he fears settling down.<br /><br />But then Jeff's window-watching clues him in to something -- sickly Mrs. Thorwald vanishes, and her husband Lars (Raymond Burr) is seen acting suspiciously with a saw, rope and metal case. Jeff becomes convinced that Thorwald has murdered his wife. He manages to convince Lisa and his down-to-earth nurse Stella (Thelma Ritter), but detectives won't believe him. So without moving from the room, Jeff uses the rear window to watch Thorwald -- and find out what really happened.<br /><br />Okay, peeping on your neighbors is not just creepy, it's illegal. In the case of \\"Rear Window,\\" that fact doesn't really matter. Watching the fellow tenants is as much fun as the mystery itself, whether it's the newlyweds, the pair that sleep on the balcony, the weepy Ms. Lonelyheart, or the buxom dancer Miss Torso. It makes the story even more chilling when you realize that one -- or maybe more than one -- of these seemingly harmless people is a murderer.<br /><br />Hitchcock -- who appears as a musician -- kept his deft touch in a movie that could have sunk like a stone. All the action takes place in one room, but he keeps it from feeling confining. Instead, the minimalistic set takes away all distractions, and makes the interplay between the characters even brighter. And much of the humor is provided by Ritter -- she's not a comic character, but her homespun wisdom is delivered with tart humor.<br /><br />Jeff is likable as only James Stewart could make him -- this guy is bored, crabby and in denial about his feelings for Lisa, but he's likable despite that. Kelly does an equally solid job as the \\"girl who is too good for him,\\" who also proves that in a pinch she can rise beyond her uptown-girl roots. Back when many women were relegated to side roles, Lisa gets to be an equal detective to Stewart.<br /><br />\\"Rear Window\\" gives a view into one of Hitchcock's best films, a taut thriller about how, if you watch other people, you might see something dangerous. I talked to my GI doc and he said that I would be asking for more discomfort if I tried this diet any longer. So I dropped it. Oh well, back to the drawing board.

The film tells the tale of a man (Jake Gyllenhall), who wakes up on a train. He has no idea why he's there or who the woman across from him is. As he's trying to get things sorted out, an explosion goes off, killing everyone. He then comes to inside a capsule where it's explained to him that the train was blown up in a terrorist bombing. The terrorist was kind enough to do a practice run and apparently plans to detonate a dirty bomb in Chicago. Due to some sort of \\"quantum\\" effect he can \\"leap\\" into the body of a man who died on the train and live out his last eight minutes of life. Doing that will, with luck, enable him to find the bomber.    If it's a premise that sounds familiar, that's because it is. It's perhaps no surprise that Scott Bakula's voice turns up in a cameo during the movie, since it clearly owes a lot to his most famous work. In fact, it's basically Quantum Leap mashed-up with Groundhog Day while managing to be better than the first and at least as engaging as the second.    There was really nothing I disliked about this film. In fact, I'd go so far as to say it's the best film I've seen so far this year, and, sadly, will likely be the best science fiction film for quite some time.

On the other hand, while I also understand that we all read into a book and take away from a book things that directly relate to our individual experiences, I feel compelled to comment on some of the complaints. Life isn't always ice cream socials.  Stuff happens, things much worse than what was in this book.  Perhaps these readers were after a heart-warming dog story.  If they had finished the book, they might have been rewarded.  It was heartwarming in spite of the tragedies and the Disney ending. Yet, I have seen dogs do some pretty amazing things that make it clear that sometimes there may be more understanding there than we imagine.     For me, the pleasure I derived from this book came from Enzo's comments about life, his solutions to problems, his own frailties and lapses in control, and from his honesty.  \\"Maybe I'm not ready to be a human, after all,\\" he laments after witnessing the extremes of human emotion, and the way Denny handles himself throughout his trials. B004NSULHM|2.0|If Thom Yorke stuck his head in an oh so deep mud puddle and blew real hard into the murky depths would the resultant recorded sound be considered brilliant music by the Radiohead faithful? I suspect so. And that my dears is the problem with Radiohead in 2011 and the new album The King of Limbs. Clocking in at under 38 minutes and with a scant 8 tracks this would have been a nice book-end companion to Yorke's failed solo album The Eraser. At this stage in their career Radiohead can put out just about anything and it will be gobbled up by the faithful like mother's milk. So, I'll confess, I'm an OK Computer Radiohead guy. I love that album. Give me some more guitar fellas, and leave out all the incessant crackling, blipping, and noodling. Much ado about nothing, I say. But let's get down to a track by track assessment to be fair. Bloom starts things off not badly, except for the annoying crackling and popping nonsense built into the percussive effects. A bit of almost classical piano, horns and a trancey feel to this tune. It's OK. Morning Mr. Magpie is more interesting, funky and the guitars chime. I think the way Yorke sings in a cooing way is rather fetching. I quite enjoy the lines \\"You Stole it, give it back\\" -- or something like that. Further along \\"You know you should, but you don't\\" is sung in an out there way that is appealing on a number of levels. The birds chirping at the end -- if those are real birds -- beats computerized bleeps and beeps any day. The next track Little by Little also has guitar, which is encouraging. I like the trippy vibe on this one. Feral is, well, feral. Actually this \\"song\\" is really brutal and epitomizes what's wrong with Radiohead these days. Repetitive nonsense and noise. Press skip and move on. Lotus Flower, which I guess is the single, shows the album moving in a better direction. The reverb-drenched chorus is cool. But this track does meander a bit musically and lyrically. Codex is suberb! The piano is lovely, as are the strings. The song is a slow burner. Yorke intones fairly sweetly on Codex: \\"Glide on...jump off the edge into a cool lake.\\" Indeed; more please! At this point the album has me hooked-in again, briefly. Give Up the Ghost is a wondrous thing. There's acoustic guitar on this song. Like Codex this one just seems to evolve as it goes. The layering of the vocals is particularly effective on this tune and it has an almost, dare I say it, pastoral feel. Separator, the album closer, is a return to the same rather ordinary beats. Not bad, I guess. So 9 albums in Radiohead seems to have found itself in a deep rut and it's one that they may never get out of. You know I can't understand why a band like Radiohead -- with such a thin catalogue of really good music -- can still sell millions of albums while a truly great band like Australia's The Church languishes in relative obscurity. Folks, if you are reading this review and you like guitar-based alternative rock check out The Church's brilliant 23 studio albums. Now, there's a band with legs and enduring greatness.

B0002KZAKS|5.0|Using this with the cheap but good quality pre-amp from ART (around $30), see my review there as well.  Before this purchase I didn't know a whole lot about the various types of Microphones and cabling and pre amps and amps and balanced versus unbalanced but finally have a setup that works though requires a pre-amp. Some things to consider:    1. Use a pre-amp for any dynamic cardiod mic (like Behringer xm8500). Low impedance microphones like xm8500 require pre-amps or perform better. Without the pre-amp, I had to turn my receiver full max to barely get any sound from it and I was afraid I was going to pop/blow the speakers if I switched inputs by mistake     2. Phantom power is NOT required for this Mic.  This is not a condensor mic     3. Use XLR male to XLR female cable between Mic and pre-amp. I had ordered XLR to 1/4 headphone jack cable and even though it physically connected to the 1/4 input on the pre-amp, the gain was not sufficient (has to do with impedance, balanced versus unbalanced and so on)     4. The output from the pre-amp can be either 1/4 or XLR, I am using XLR to 1/4 cable (I had one so why not) and then a 1/4 female to RCA into my receiver. Of course if I wanted to, I could use a different connector to plug into my computer and record if I need to (haven't tried it yet)     Now that I know this, it all seems so trivial but I hope it helps someone who wants to just setup a decent Mic for vocals cheaply. The Mic is flawless and I am quite happy with it.  It seems good quality for the price.    Happy singing.

B003TXSF5C|3.0|First, I am a big fan of Stephen Hawking.  I've followed his theories since I was in high schol (back when he could still speak on his own).  A Brief History of Time was absolutely wonderful.  I had high hopes for The Grand Design.  Unfortunately, I came away from it feeling somewhat disappointed.<br /><br />I am not a physicist or mathematecian.  But I am fairly educated, and I anticipate that Hawking wrote this book not for the professional physicist, but rather for the mass market fan of science and physics (yes, those people actually exist).  I have a rudimentary working knowledge of physics and math, so I wouldn't say the book was over my head, although some parts of it were daunting.<br /><br />My biggest problem is that Hawking spends most of the book describing past theories of the universe, such as the Ancient Greek, or Mayan civilization, or religious theories, and builds up to being able to refute them through science.  He never gets there.  It is impossible to prove that God did not create the universe, and Hawking does not claim this.  He only claims that it is not necessary to involve God in a theory when science is perfectly capable of explaining everything.  Here is the spoiler:  The universe we experience is only one of 10 to the 500th power of the number of universes that can exist, and we just happen to have developed on a planet with a certain eliptical orbit and distance from a sun/star of a certain power at a certain period of time in history.  All of this combines to form the neccessary elements of life, and evolution takes it from there.  However, all of the theories seem to be assumptions built upon assumptions.  For example, quarks are the building blocks of subatomic particles such as neutrons and protons.  However, quarks cannot be isolated.  Thus, sub atomic particles, such as quarks, mesons, bosons, etc. are mathematical principles used to explain anomolies in the data.  We assume they exist because doing so fits the data.  Also, he relies heavily on Feynmann's theories of quantum physics.  In the famous double-slit test, Hawking describes that the pattern of interference lead Feynmann to believe that the particles, or \\"bucky-balls\\" don't just take both paths through the slits, but they take all possible paths.  This is the basis of Feynmann's \\"alternative histories\\" theory.  However, bucky-balls are 60 carbon atom balls, which on the grand scheme of atomic physics, is like a beach ball.  Why can't we create bucky-balls using a radioative carbon, like carbon 13 or carbon 14 and use a radiographic analysis to see where the particles actually go, instead of theorizing that they go everywhere in the universe at once?<br /><br />At the end of the day, Hawking states that the universes come into and out of existence spontaneously, as long as the energies balance out correctly.  It is in this wehre our universe has been formed.  We are a statistical anaomoly.  Also, while the big bang is assumed to be true, we cannot study the big bang itself because it is a singularity.  So again, we assume it.  I certainly am not advocating that its the big bang or God, but I don't think Hawking goes far enough to prove his thesis, and in postulating spontaneous generation of universes, leaves the eager reader feeling somewhat underwhelmed.eynmann's theories of quantum physics.  In the famous double-slit test, Hawking describes that the pattern of interference lead Feynmann to believe that the particles, or \\"bucky-balls\\" don't just take both paths through the slits, but they take all possible paths.  This is the basis of Feynmann's \\"alternative histories\\" theory.  However, bucky-balls are 60 carbon atom balls, which on the grand scheme of atomic physics, is like a beach ball.  Why can't we create bucky-balls using a radioative carbon, like carbon 13 or carbon 14 and use a radiographic analysis to see where the particles actually go, instead of theorizing that they go everywhere in the universe at once?    At the end of the day, Hawking states that the universes come into and out of existence spontaneously, as long as the energies balance out correctly.  It is in this wehre our universe has been formed.  We are a statistical anaomoly.  Also, while the big bang is assumed to be true, we cannot study the big bang itself because it is a singularity.  So again, we assume it.  I certainly am not advocating that its the big bang or God, but I don't think Hawking goes far enough to prove his thesis, and in postulating spontaneous generation of universes, leaves the eager reader feeling somewhat underwhelmed.

B001PBRCVI|5.0|July 1942 marked a dark period in the history of France where thousands of Jewish families were rounded up and forcibly kept in the Velodrome d'Hiver. They were then sent off to transit camps in France such as Drancy, before being packed off to Auschwitz, a Nazi death camp. What is disturbing about this whole incident is that the rounding up and mobilization of Jews for deportation was done by the French authorities.<br /><br />Based upon this seldom mentioned, little known piece of French history, author Tatiana de Rosnay has crafted a well-written novel that alternates between the past in 1942, and the present. The past centers around a 10 year old Jewish girl Sarah Strazynski who is forced to go to the Velodrome d'Hiver with her mother and father, innocently leaving behind a 4 year old brother Michel locked in a secret cupboard with the assurance that she would return to let him out when it was safe.<br /><br />The present revolves around writer Julia Jarmond, a transplanted American who is married to a frenchman and finds herself being consumed by the story of the Vel d'Hiv incident. As she digs deeper, she uncovers dark secrets surrounding her husband's family which are connected to the deportations of Jews from France. As the truth emerges, the author deftly handles the question of guilt caused by supressed secrets and how the truth can sometimes not only bring about pain and disrupt the regularity of life, yet also have the ability to heal and move forwards into the future.<br /><br />The method employed by the author, which alternates between the past [1942] and the present is an effective tool for it ties both periods together and brings the story to a satisfying conclusion. I do confess though that I found the story of the past much more dramatic and interesting than the one which deals with Julia in the present. On the whole though, it was an engrossing read and I would recommend it, especially to those interested in the genre. I'd also recommend the following books which deal with the Holocaust and France: \\"The Holocaust, the French, and the Jews\\" by Susan Zuccoti (non-fiction), \\"One Step Ahead of Hitler: A Jewish Child's Journey Through France\\" by Fred Gross (memoir), and \\"France:The Dark Years, 1940-1944\\" by Julian Jackson (non-fiction and more for those already familiar with the history of the period).ith the Holocaust and France: \\"The Holocaust, the French, and the Jews\\" by Susan Zuccoti (non-fiction), \\"One Step Ahead of Hitler: A Jewish Child's Journey Through France\\" by Fred Gross (memoir), and \\"France:The Dark Years, 1940-1944\\" by Julian Jackson (non-fiction and more for those already familiar with the history of the period).

Then, people began asking me if I had read it; and, when I responded in the negative, they proceeded to rave about it, using phrases like, \\"deeply disturbing\\", \\"amazing\\", \\"moving\\", and \\"powerful\\".   \\"Wow\\", I thought, \\"I need to read this.\\"    And so I did. Mid-way through the book, when readers finally see our main characters leave the room is one of the most unbelievable, sloppy plot contrivances I've ever seen. The mother, previously dependent upon her son to the point of obsessiveness (as evidenced by the breastfeeding) seems to resent her son and to want to be separated from him.    On Thursday, 19 May, I finished the book; and promptly wished for the time I spent reading it back.  Cursory research showed me that the plot had been based on several well-publicized cases involving kidnappings and abductions. This made me snort, as there is a line in the book indicating that those who profit on the tragedies of others are heartless.  Oh, the irony. B003V4AK8E|4.0|Pro Elite Wireless Controller for PS3  Let me start out by saying that if you're one of the crazy people with baby hands out there who loves the Dual Shock 3, steer clear of this controller and stop reading this review. If you're like me, however, and you like to use a controller that doesn't add a barrier between you and the video games you love so much, continue on.  I'll go on record and say that the Xbox 360 controller is the best controller ever designed by man (minus the horrific D-pad). It fits perfectly in the hands, all controls are within reach, triggers function flawlessly, etc. After a minute or two, you forget you're holding a controller and it stays that way until you put it down. The Dual Shock 3 controller is possibly the worst designed controller on this planet. I've heard people say that the N64 controller deserves that award, but I never owned an N64, so can't confirm or deny that. The Dual Shock 3's thumbsticks are awkwardly placed and seem to be coated with Teflon. The triggers are worthless (which explains why all games use L1 and R1 for shooting related duties). The controller itself is entirely too small, unless you have baby hands. Constant adjustment is required to attempt to keep it comfortable, although it's ultimately futile. Fingers are bunched up in the back due to lack of room. Pushing the L and R buttons requires super-dexterity if controlling the thumbsticks. I could go on and on. Suffice it to say, the person that designed the DS3 should probably be whipped with 1,000 USB cables.  Along comes the Pro Elite Wireless Controller for PS3. They've essentially tried to copy the design of the 360 controller, although not quite to a T. Overall the controller feels pretty good in your hands. The matte black finish is nice. The build quality seems good. The left thumbstick is where it should be, off to the side. The face buttons have a nice backlight that can be turned on or off, a nice little touch. The triggers are infinitely better than on the DS3. Unfortunately, most games force you to use R1 to shoot and don't give an option to change that. It's pretty annoying having decent triggers at your disposal, and having no choice but to use the buttons above them to fire a gun. I wish Power A would have taken this into consideration and perhaps put switches on the controller allowing you to swap the 1s and 2s.  The Pro Elite is definitely a win and a huge step up from the DS3, but it's not without flaws. I'm not a huge fan of the grip where your palms rest, but you get used to it. The placement of the right thumbstick is a little farther inside than it probably should be, making it a bit of a reach if you have smaller hands. The controller requires a dongle connected to the console, which seems silly, but maybe it's the only way to do it without Sony's blessing. This prevents you from turning the system on with the controller. It's a little bit on the light side. Some heft would make it feel a bit more solid, but that's a preference thing. Lastly, my biggest gripe is with the thumbsticks. The dead zones are a bit larger than they should be. It's not a deal-breaker, but it's definitely noticeable.  In closing, if you like games and hate the DS3, based on my research it looks like this is probably the best you can get, short of building Ben Heck's PS360 controller (or better yet, paying him to do it). Some slight modifications on the design, fixing the dead zone issues and making it Bluetooth and this controller would be a perfect 10. As of right now, though, it's only about an 8. Which is still good, but not perfect.

0307887960|2.0|I wanted to make sure that, before reviewing this book, I had completed several weeks of the diet, in order to actually speak to the results.  Having read it, gone through the first \\"Attack\\" phase (3-5 days of nothing but lean protein, pinpointed more accurately on the Dukan website), and transitioned to the second phase (a month or so of lean protein alternated with lean protein+veggies), I can say that the diet did not work for me as it was offered.  To be more accurate, I GAINED five lbs on the Attack phase, which is billed as a \\"radical transformation in your body,\\" and as a rapid weight loss phase.  So here are the reasons I think this was not the diet for me:    - I am a vegetarian.  Dukan devotes several pages to explaining how vegetarians can follow the diet as well as anyone, and even recommends specific foods.  So I went right out and bought gobs of those recommended foods, such as nonfat cottage cheese, morningstar farms grillers, etc.  And they were delicious!  Eating those things was no problem, at least until I got pretty bored with them.  Even then, I ate nothing but what he had listed (the above, plus egg whites, very low fat string cheese, sugar free treats, low sugar soy milk, Greek yogurt with a tablespoon of oat bran everyday, made one attempt to made the disgusting oat galette, etc).    - I have hit a weight loss plateau, after losing 75lbs (from 220lbs to 145lbs) a few years ago.  I gained a little bit back in the years since, and was looking to trim and tone from this diet, especially after a long and indulgent winter.  For me, it's really hard to figure out how to cut any more calories from my day without being exhausted or feeling famished, mainly because...    - I workout.  A LOT.  The Attack phase definitely left me too tired and feeling woozy to do my normal routine of 45mins of cardio and 30-60mins of circuit weight training every day, so that week I skipped the gym and instead walked the dog at least 50 minutes a day.  Still, I found myself getting winded at the slightest walk up my stairs, and my muscles just didn't want to work.  Even worse, my brain just felt sluggish.  High protein diets are staples of body builders (research shows they help burn fat and gain muscle), but without glucose provided by veggies or carbs, it's really hard to get your body fueled properly.  I spoke to some trainers at the gym (one with a PhD in exercise physiology), and they all strongly recommended that I at LEAST add veggies and fruit to the diet.  Thus, after the Attack phase was over (3 days according to the Dukan site, but I gave it 5 days because I didn't see any results), I took their advice and started eating lean protein+veggies+fruit.  Dukan's prescribed diet just wasn't for me.    HOWEVER, in the long run, I DID change my eating habits.  I was eating a lot of bagels and drinking a few glasses of wine with dinner every night before I started the Dukan diet.  The radical shift that happened when I ate nothing but lean protein all day (Attack phase) was enough to make me realize that I wasn't getting enough protein.  In addition, by snacking on a lot of carbs all day, I was creating a lot of blood sugar spikes and false feelings of hunger, even after having eaten 1500+ calories for the day.  So one of the nice take-aways I had from this book was the shift from carb-based eating to protein-based eating.  I'm making significantly more of an effort to eat tons of lean protein, and I HAVE lost 7 lbs since the Attack phase ended.  I can't say it's all the Dukan plan, however, since I eat all the veggies I want everyday, as well as fruit and even the (very) occasional [[ASIN:B003NTZ9LU Arnold's Select Sandwich Thins - 100% Whole Wheat, 8 ct. bag (Pack of 4)]].  My body needs more than the Dukan plan offers, but responds well to the significant reduction in carbs that's more consistent with the [[ASIN:1439190275 New Atkins for a New You: The Ultimate Diet for Shedding Weight and Feeling Great.]].    So, all and all, I think it might be great for some people (probably best for meat eaters).  I count calories everyday as a routine on my iPhone, and I can say it's well nigh impossible to get more than 1400 calories everyday from lean protein.  You feel STUFFED by about 1100 calories, which is nice.  If you can handle the side effects of tiredness, bad breath, food boredom, and (at least for me) a possible increase in weight in the beginning, this could be a long-term solution for you, simply because you will find it so hard to eat more calories than you burn on a daily basis.  I also can say that, by avoiding all carbohydrates, my IBS and lactose sensitivity just disappeared, leading me to do more research into cutting gluten out of my diet completely.  If you like structure and can handle a huge shift in your eating habits, this is the plan for you.  I can only offer up my experience, though, and say that by adding back all of the fruits and veggies during the \\"Cruise\\" phase (really, by just skipping to a modified Stabilization phase, similar to what he suggested for teenagers and children), I was a LOT happier with both the efforts and the results. However, this isn't what he recommends (he constantly reminds you to follow the diet to the letter), so I can't review the diet any higher than two stars.

B00466HN6S|3.0|(Written by the teenager in residence)    Upon first hearing of Hop, I had high hopes for it. Although the premise - holiday figure gets injured in accident, everyman has to replace him - has been done before, one detail caught my eye - \\"From the people who brought you Despicable Me\\". Despicable Me is one of my favorite modern-day animated films, with interesting (and entertaining) characters, solid plotting, and a good dose of heart. Naturally, I expected this movie to be on the same level, or at least have some solid execution. It delivered in the entertainment value, but it left a lot to be desired.      Story: 7/10 - As mentioned, the premise combines several cliches - kid has legacy, kid wants to be something else, kid runs away from home, is in danger of having his birthright or whatever stolen by some other guy if he doesn't make it back home before a certain date. It also has the standard everyman who can't hold a job, but has his life changed when he accidentally injures, maims, or even kills a holiday figure (in this case it's the former) and eventually finds his place in the world. It's been done a million times, and the fact that said holiday figure is a talking CGI animal and the humans are all in live-action draws parallels to Alvin and the Chipmunks.    Depth: 4/10 - Now, a cliched premise is not a bad thing, and some works have managed to excel despite (or because of) their uses of cliches (like BlazBlue: Calamity Trigger), although mileage varies. A bungled execution, however, is certainly not good. The interactions between the human protagonist(Fred o'Hare) and the rabbit (E.B.) don't have much of an emotional impact - it's same old, same old. There are some interesting plot points that never get explored further - E.B. wants to be a drummer, rather than the Easter Bunny, but we're never really given a clear reason why (except for \\"touring the world\\", but his father (the incumbent Easter Bunny) points out that easter bunnies go all over the world,too). The Pink Berets, sent to bring E.B. back home, aren't developed, don't have a single line of dialogue, and are generally incompetent (they mistake a Butterball for a bunny by the end). And the fact that the easter bunnies live on Easter Island could have been used for some interesting, if far-fetched, plot points - maybe the Moai are actually sentient guardians of the factory where the Easter Bunnies make their candy? But no - all of these plots are wasted.    Characters: 5/10 - Most of these characters are underdeveloped and underused throughout the movie. For example, Fred fits squarely into the archetype of \\"beleagured slacker with a good heart\\", and doesn't show much depth except for his desire to be the Easter Bunny. To his credit, he does show that he's willing to work for his goals, but that's really it. The main villain, Carlos, is an obvious bad guy with cliched motives, but he is the most sympathetic character - considering how big of a jerk the Easter Bunny is when Carlos tries to show that he can be competent, it's easy to understand why he's so ticked off. The sad thing, though, is that we never see that Carlos could be a good easter bunny - indeed, when he takes over, he almost ruins the holiday experience for non-birds. All of the human characters except for Fred and David Hasselhoff (don't ask) are underutilized as well - his older sister, Sam, is used to give him a job interview that E.B. fumbles for him and a lecture on responsibility, but doesn't really do anything else. His younger sister, Alex, acts like a jerk and hassles Fred during a family dinner, but ends up upstaged and humiliated by a \\"ventriloquism\\" act that comes across as disproportionate karma. The whole scene doesn't have that much of an impact on the main plot, either, except for straining Fred's realtionship with his family even further. And don't get me started on the Pink Berets - seriously, don't.     Entertainment: 8/10 - Despite these glaring flaws, Hop proves to be entertaining on the first go-through regardless. It's only when examined with a keen eye that the fridge logic sets in. The jokes are relatively funny, the animation is fluid and avoids uncanny valley, and the soundtrack is decent, although not magnificent. The MST3K mantra - \\"It's only a movie, I should really relax\\" - applies in full here, but it still manages to be a nice diversion overall.     Overall: 24/40 - Like I said, there are many flaws and cliches used in this movie. There are a lot of wasted plots and potential characterization gone down the drain, but it still manages to be a nice experience on the first go-through. My recommendation would be to see it once, laugh a lot, and don't think too much about it. For the kiddies, take them to see \\"Rango\\" - a vastly superior animated experience - or watch \\"Despicable Me\\" again before you go see this. It's a good time on the first go-through, but overall, it's just... meh.

B00007KWHP|5.0|With The Yes Album behind and Close to the Edge ahead, Fragile lies between two tall musical peaks. Recorded at the end of 1971 with Rick Wakeman as a newcomer, this is an album which surpassed anyone's expectations - something big came together when Yes entered the studio to record it.     Fragile mixed individual ideas - five songs, one for each member with four full blown group contributions, something which lent a distinctive flavour to it and ultimately gave more power to their creative differences. This is also the absolute peak of Jon Anderson's voice in my opinion while Chris Squire, Steve Howe and Bill Bruford are all at the top of their creativity and sound truly inspired all through the record with Rick Wakeman's colourful ideas as the icing of the cake. Everything blends into a highly inventive and most unique experience which was surely unheard of at the time of its release. 1971 was a most unbelievable year in music when defining albums such as this, Led Zeppelin IV and Jethro Tull's Aqualung - among many others - were recorded.    Although Yes would still fly high for the next few years, with Tales From Topographic Oceans being one of my all-time favourites, this is where they reached this musical brilliance and complexity for the first time. Songs such as Roundabout, South Side Of The Sky, Long Distance Runaround and Heart Of The Sunrise are all classic Yes without a doubt, and would stand out on any album by anyone for that matter.       As for the Rhino remastered 2003 reissue, I can only say that this was/is the release you'd been waiting for. Great sound allied to great packaging can only bring great results and that's exactly what you get here.

1400063736|5.0|Words exist to communicate.  They carry an idea from one brain to another.  Sometimes they are not very good at their job.  For example, sometimes we are left guessing just exactly what someone meant when they said, \\"We all know what the problem is here.\\"     But other times words are incredibly efficient--for example, when someone yells \\"Fire!\\" and everyone runs out of the building.  In this particular instance, that one word carries a lot of information.  It manages to say, \\"To all of you who can hear me right now, there is a fire in this structure.  It is a dangerous thing.  If you can hear me you should get out of this structure as quickly as you can.  You should also tell others of this danger.\\"  As a ratio of meaning to words, \\"Fire!\\" packs quite a wallop.     On the train on the way to Philadelphia today I finished reading Colum McCann's kick-in-the-stomach collection of interconnected short stories called Let the Great World Spin.  It got me thinking about words and their efficiency.  Edgar Allen Poe had a theory about short stories.  He believed that a short story should be about one precise feeling or effect and every word in the story should contribute to that effect.  Even the most lyrical and beautiful of sentences should be cut if it got in the way of the feeling the author hoped to create in the reader's soul.     Somehow, in his 349-page collection of eleven stories centered on the true-life walk of Philippe Petit from one of the World Trade Center towers to the other on an August morning in 1974, Colum McCann manages the literary equivalent of yelling \\"Fire!\\" Taken as a whole, the stories of the twelve characters in Let the Great World Spin manage to convey the full range of what it means to be a thinking, feeling human in the world.  This book is miraculous.     It is one of the most efficient books I have ever read.  The meaning-to-word ratio is huge.  Some of my favorite sentences are below:      \\"No shame in saying that I felt a loneliness drifting through me.  Funny how it was, everyone perched in their own little world, with the deep need to talk,  each person with their own tale, beginning in some strange middle point, then trying so hard to tell it all, to have it all make sense, logical and final.\\"    \\"I guess this is what marriage is, or was, or could be.  You drop the mask. You allow the fatigue in.  You lean across and kiss the years because they're the things that matter.\\"    \\"She likes the people with the endurance to tolerate the drudge, the ones who know that pain is a requirement, not a curse.\\"    \\"The only thing worth grieving over, she said, was that sometimes there was more beauty in this life than the world could bear.\\"       And sometimes more pain.  And somehow Colum McCann has taken both--as well as everything in between--and put it to just the right words to say it all.  And more.    \\"The world spins.  We stumble on.  It is enough.\\"     [...]

B000SEGTI0|1.0|DO ANDROIDS DREAM OF ELECTRIC SHEEP is a very poorly executed dystopia, and it's all due to the soullessness of PKD's writing. The mass extinction of animals, the decay of all matter into \\"kibble,\\" and the abandonment of Earth by a majority of humans to off-world colonies all underscore the bleakness of the future.<br /><br />However, the effect is lost on the reader because there is no one that warrants empathy. With the exception of the pathetic J. R. Isidore, the characters, androids and humans alike, are automatons: boring and lifeless. Conversations are completely flat and unaffected. They don't talk to each other, but rather past each other, so they hardly react to what is going on around them. Blink, and the reader will miss a startling revelation. There's no natural break to reflect, \\"Oh, my gosh, she really is an android!\\" or \\"Holy cow, their religion is a fraud!\\" What's worse, these revelations are mere unpopped kernels of philosophical insight that PKD takes no pains to explore.<br /><br />The reader is sometimes told how the characters feel, but PKD never shows us how they feel, or offers to explain why. There's no deep explanation behind Rick Deckard's moodiness; he's just moody. He buys a black Nubian goat on impulse and has sex with an android on a whim--there's no indication he even enjoyed it. He changes his mind in the middle of a conversation (\\"I won't kill her/Maybe I will\\"), but the reader has no clue where he will come out, because we have no idea what he's thinking.<br /><br />The plot is terribly straightforward but mired in wild, incongruous details, like Deckard being kidnapped by a shadow police unit that, unbeknownst to itself, is comprised entirely of androids. This would appear to be a game-changer, but Deckard forgets it ever happened. There's also the paradoxical Voigt-Kampf test: not only can Deckard not rely on it to tell androids apart from humans, but the test itself is unnecessary, as Deckard already knows the names of the androids he's looking for.<br /><br />PKD's laconic prose is as soulless as the characters. I don't mean the text is descriptive enough. I mean it presents events in a monotonous, minimalist cadence that fails to convey any sense of excitement or urgency. Whereas the final battle with Roy Baty in the movie included a 10-minute chase culminating in a poetic rooftop eulogy, the novel's climax is literally wrapped up in a single paragraph.<br /><br />The only redeeming trait is the character of J. R. Isidore, whose brain is defective due to fallout sickness. He captures a spider in his apartment building and is sickened when the androids he is helping to hide from Deckard begin dissecting it while it was still alive. This is a scene where the \\"less is more\\" writing style actually works, because we can posit our own reaction on J. R. Isidore. Too bad the rest of the novel could not have carried the same moral presence.PKD's laconic prose is as soulless as the characters. I don't mean the text is descriptive enough. 1907590102|4.0|I have been curious about this book for a long time, both because of the Bill Murray movie and [[ASIN:0099468646 Earthly Powers]], Burgess' book featuring a fictionalized version of Maugham as well as an ending featuring a destructive cult that may be a rumination on the spirituality of Larry. Finding out that the Larry character is based on Christopher Isherwood makes it all the more interesting (although Isherwood's dabbling with Eastern mysticism didn't make him asexual and his later years were lived in blissful cohabitation with a man 30 years younger).     So I was a little startled to discover that Larry is not the main character of the book. Larry is merely a template for Maugham to meditate on his view of America which is a country that Maugham sees as capable of embracing everything with an enthusiasm - whether it's materialism or spirituality. Larry is the spiritualist side of the American spirit with his wanderings in every conceivable occupation including coal miner and Hindu mystic but his former fiancee represents the materialism that will always be chasing after the spiritual and having it elude him. In between is Sophia, the woman that Larry gets engaged to as a means of saving her soul even as Isabel connives to destroy her. She works as a creature of excess who has potential for grace or self-destruction.    I was not prepared for the Maugham filter in which everything the main character (Larry) does is filtered through the lens of Maugham (writing as a character in the story) who is world weary and cynical and therefore taken with Larry's mystical air of a man cut off from the world and seeking meaning beyond the vulgar materialism. As far as Maugham's narrative is concerned, Larry's wandering and wondering is as foregin to his sensibilities as the \\"money money money\\" attitudes of the rest of the characters. The only one that Maugham really feels a kinship with is Elliot the snob who is self-consciously being a snob to compensate for not being oneof the idle rich. What's even more interesting is the way that Maugham can see that Larry's rejected fiancee (she wants him to be normal but WWI changed him) Isabel is a vicious woman without scruple - and finds it obvious that she left an open bottle of alcohol for the tragic Sophie to indulge in when she was attempting to stay sober. Maugham doesn't judge her because he understands her, but he also knows that Larry wouldn't be able to notice her sins.     I was hoping for more about post-WWI veterans and the lost generation, but this is not a lost generation novel. It is a work of fiction entrenched in the drawing rooms of the pre-war era and seeking something for more.

B0049P1ZZQ|5.0|The Walking Dead Season 1 Blu-ray Review    Distributor: Anchor Bay Entertainment (US)    Some of us have been waiting patiently for a full blown zombie television series for what seems like an eternity and finally after many years of rumours and speculation we finally get Frank Darabont's The Walking Dead.    So, would the wait eventually prove to be worthwhile? Actually, it probably was.    The series begins with the story of a police officer shot during his regular day to day activities, only to awake in hospital some weeks later to find that in the meantime the world he knew has simply gone to Hell, quite literally. The story progresses across 6 episodes and deals with a handful of survivors battling hordes of the undead whilst trying to find a safe haven and avoid becoming the next zombie meal. With a fair amount of character background which builds across the various relationships, the struggle for survival brings a decent amount of tension to the screen with characters that you will actually care for and a couple of reasonably well placed twists along the way to keep you guessing. The gore factor is also pretty high for a television series and the effects look altogether excellent throughout.    Worth noting in fact, is that the gore looked so realistic, that in all countries other than the original US broadcasts, edits have been applied to a couple of the episodes during their major gore scenes. This also remains true of the Blu-ray releases as every other version apart from this US disc contains the cut print, so if you want to see the full version of the episodes, you will be needing this US release from Anchor Bay. The only problem there being is that this disc is unfortunately locked to region A. More proof if any was required, that a region free Blu-ray player is the only way to go.     Anchor Bay have released The Walking Dead with an excellent 1080p 1.78:1 wide-screen transfer which looks fantastic. Detail is superb with colours and black levels enriching the performance. Having watched the original broadcasts, the Blu-ray looks to be on a different level and really immerses you into the story. With no print damage on display and enough grain to enhance the atmosphere and still give that home made quality, this really is the ultimate way to view the series.    Audio is also excellent. Presented with a True HD 5.1 soundtrack there is plenty here to grab your attention. From the moaning hordes of re-animated corpses to the bullet ridden showdowns, the audio drags you kicking and screaming into the action. Dialogue is clean and crisp, explosions are bassy and powerful and combined with the already classic music score, an altogether brilliant performance is delivered throughout.    Extras are also of an excellent quality, including The Making of The Walking Dead which provides a 30 minute documentary delving into various behind-the-scenes features and interviews with the cast and crew. Following this there are six mini featurettes which each focus on one particular episode in turn, behind the scenes make up tests, a producers Q&A session with a Comic-Con setting and a couple of zombie specific supplements. Rounding all of this off, there are also a couple of cast interviews and an on camera tour of one of the shows sets.    All things considered, this set is a must have for zombie fans everywhere and although some parts of the series share more than a passing similarity to films like 28 days later and modern day zombie remakes such as the Dawn of the Dead, the story here is both fun filled and gripping with more than enough new concepts to please all but the most demanding horror fan. With Season 2 beginning later this year, there is no better time to revisit the first season again, especially in its new enhanced presentation.      \\"WHAT MAKES US HUMAN?\\"      BLU REVIEW OBSCURA - for those less mainstream Blu-ray reviews - find us in Facebook

First impressions:    Upside  1. Gives the videographer much more creative control over the audio portion of the shoot.  Compared to the on-board 5.1 mike array (including when it is set for front-stereo pick up only via the camcorder), the VideoMic rolls off to-the-side audio (e.g., comments by the camcorder operator or from a noisy background) much more effectively so the subject's sounds are recorded crisply.  2. The gain/level control adjustments (-10dB, flat, + 20dB) coupled with the camcorder's on-screen audio volume meter or using the real-time audio output and earphones greatly help with balancing far away and near-field subject audio so you can record more precisely.  3. The rubber shock and isolation mounts effectively remove extraneous camcorder sounds (by the way, this camcorder doesn't have appreciable fan noise, contrary to a few negative comments about it).  4. It's compact and doesn't enter into the field of view of the camcorder even in its widest angle mode.    Downside  1. The camera's special audio functions, such as changing the angle of response proportional to zoom, won't work with the external mike, but the manual level settings help with this.  2. One more thing to stuff into the camera bag, but just get a bigger bag.  3. No case for the device, so get a ziploc bag to prevent damage or loss of the rubber bands during transport.    I haven't tried the high-pass/low-cut filter for suppression of noise from fast wind, but using just the supplied foam wind shield (not a dead cat), preliminary tests with < 10 mph wind shows effectiveness.     Overall, this improves my confidence in the audio quality of the shoot.

Linger is about Sam, a boy who was once a wolf, and Grace, the girl he has loved since he saved her from the wolves when they were both children. As their relationship progresses, Sam is dealing with the trauma in his past, and Grace is dealing with her parents, and trying to figure out why she is so sick all of a sudden. In Shiver, the book is told through Sam and Grace's points of view (POV). In Linger, Sam and Grace are still prominent characters, but Stiefvater adds two additional POV. One is Cole, a singer in a very popular band who decided to become a wolf to escape his pain, and the other is Isabel, whose brother became a wolf in Shiver. I don't want to spoil anything so I am not going to say much except, THIS SERIES IS A MUST READ!! I can see myself reading these books over and over again, and have suggested this book to all of my book loving friends! The words flowed beautifully, the plot was original and exciting, and the characters were easy to relate to. The third, and final book in the trilogy is called Forever, and will come out July 12, 2011. While Shiver ended on a very happy and promising note, Stiefvater thought it would be fun to torture us and ended Linger with a major cliffhanger. I can not wait to get my hands on Forever! July can not come soon enough!
B000CCIVDO|3.0|I want to like these headphones, the sound is clean and accurate with solid bass.  Sadly, the HPS5000 is the most uncomfortable headset I've ever owned. The ear cups are small and stiff, the overhead plastic band feels like a clamp and is poorly adjustable. Ten minutes and I'm ready to settle for the ratty little speakers in my laptop.

B002MQYOFW|5.0|I once read an introduction to one of Robert E. Howard's books that stated he was a \\"natural storyteller.\\"  The writer's point was that, without the ability to tell a story, all the narrative gifts in the world are useless, just as all the trim and finish in the world can't make up for the fact that a boat doesn't float.    By golly, Suzanne Collins is a natural storyteller.  You know how I know?  Because, even though there are some shaky premises to the world she has created, I really didn't care.  For example, I never really got a good grasp on this world she's desrcibing.  It appears to be scattered all around North America, with 12 habitable zones connected by something like high-speed rail.  I never got a good sense as to why the rest of the continent was no longer inhabited, or why anything that rendered so much turf uninhabitable would have spared 12 spots.  Nor did I get a feel for the population.  I sorta got the impression that there were about 20,000 people in her home burg -- but that would give a continental population far too small to uphold the high-tech civilization we witness.  So the background was vague and implausible and didn't hold together.  But I never really cared!  There might have been problems with the setting and the sociology, but she was telling a thoroughly absorbing story, peopled with interesting characters, vivid drama and irony, genuine intrigue, and a few moments of suspense to equal any others I have ever read.  She kept me engrossed and turning pages right up to the end, and even convinced me to plunk down more of my money to buy the second volume, because it's never available at the library.      You can pick up on details of this post-apocalyptic dystopia from other reviews or the publisher's account.  But I'm here to testify that Suzanne Collins has told a really good story, and richly deserves her success.  Enjoy!
0307591549|5.0|Shawn Achor proposes this overarching principle: \\"Happiness is the center, and success revolves around it.\\" (p. 37) Achor depicts happiness like the core of a wheel, powering a smooth and speedy glide, or a lack of happiness putting a brake on the spin of success. This idea is meant to contrast with the notion that success comes first, bringing happiness in its wake.    The author is a management consultant, and he marshals an impressive spread of research from the fields of psychology and management, as well as consulting anecdotes. A witty and concise writer, this book is a joy to read as well. Overall, Shawn Achor argues that increasing people's day-to-day happiness will pay off with more successful teams, more successful organizations, and more success in people's individual careers. He offers big-picture reflections as well as a handy toolkit for increasing one's quotient of the Happiness Advantage.    The author's advice on how to increase happiness in the workplace is meant for anyone in the working world -- not just CEOs. The primary focus is on the person reading the book, sharing advice on how to boost one's own happiness in an organizational context. It speaks to \\"the ripple effect\\" too -- spreading happiness around to others, brightening the ambiance and contributing to overall success.    I like this book a lot. At the same time, I can imagine a results-driven line of skepticism that might ask, aren't we happier when we're winning? One might think that high achievement will tend to give rise to happy people on teams and happiness individually in careers. Right? Shawn Achor says no, it's the other way around -- that happiness is a driver of success in organizations and individual careers. While this book may not fully resolve a chicken-and-egg conundrum to everyone's satisfaction, it surely puts something very good on the table. Achor targets happiness as an independent factor in the workplace. He offers an impressive spread of evidence for the \\"Happiness Advantage\\" and a helpful set of practical tips and techniques. I'm happy that I read it.

B004QSQM72|5.0|My first exposure to Wild Beasts was with \\"Devil's Crayon,\\" where Tom Fleming's beautiful baritone voice is most prominent. Then I watched the video for \\"Hooting & Howling\\" and was blown away with how original Hayden Thorpe's falsetto singing is. They truly have a unique sound, and \\"Smother\\" showcases their talents. Listening to these two very different voices is just magical stuff. Much has been made of the fact that this album is a little more somber and doesn't have all the explicit sexuality of their previous two efforts, but there's still that boldness here that draws on references to gay and/or straight lovemaking as metaphors for something more that's going on in the relationships. These are far from your standard love songs. There's a lot of synthesizer work here, but a video that shows Tom and Hayden doing an acoustic version of \\"Reach a Bit Further\\" on a Paris footbridge above passing cars, shows how awesome they sound even with nothing but a single guitar as accompaniment.<br /><br />1. Lion's Share - 4:14 - Starts with a minimal synthesized beat and Hayden doing his wonderful vocal gymnastics, later the tempo builds and Tom provides gorgeous background vocal support. It's one of 2 songs on the album (the other being \\"Plaything\\") in which Hayden plays the part of the boastful lover, bragging about taking the lion's share of the relationship and of his lover's body. Elegant piano playing is mixed in with the synthesized sounds.<br /><br />2. Bed of Nails - 4:18 - Hayden on lead vocal in this mid-tempo song. It's a pronouncement of a masochistic devotion to a lover (\\"I would lie anywhere with you, any bed of nails would do\\"). Continuing in that masochistic theme, the chorus draws on the name of Hamlet's lover, Ophelia, who killed herself over his mistreatment, although in the chorus Hayden and Tom play on the sexual punning that name always invites, and even sing with a Donna Summer-esque, in-the-throes-of-ecstasy moan. (The metaphorical lines about how immersed he feels in his lover's attention are also interesting in that Ophelia drowned herself: \\"Surround me like me a warm bath, set me up like an epitaph.\\" )<br /><br />3. Deeper - 3:01 - Tom has the lead vocals in this slow tempo piece. As their songs often do, the lyrics make reference to the physical act of lovemaking while also suggesting something more metaphorical. Tom's inviting his lover to go deeper with him, realizing the risks of raising their passion -- \\"Dare we put our tongues to the flames.\\" The chorus of \\"deeper, deeper\\" with the harmonizing background vocals is just exquisite to listen to.<br /><br />4. Loop the Loop - 4:06 - A slow song with Hayden on lead vocal. As he said in an NPR interview, it's a song about human acceptance, and he says he picked up some of the lines, like \\"Forget now,\\" from watching Pedro Almodovar's \\"Women on the Verge of a Nervous Breakdown.\\" There is a chorus line about \\"people are the strangest things\\" and then he explores why in a repeating line that changes its final verb in 3 variations \\"All that the heart requires it what it can't - recognize (1st), compromise (2nd), sympathize (3rd).\\" Basically, I take it to mean we all depend on forgiveness and acceptance, but it's hard to give. The music has a soothing, almost lullaby-ish \\"loop\\" with a bongo and drum supplied rhythm.<br /><br />5. Plaything - 4:21 - Not your typical love song, and brilliant in its courageous boasting. A slow synthesized buzz starts things off, followed by a drumbeat, then Hayden comes directing his lover to \\"take off your chemise, and I'll do as I please.\\" The chorus is the repeated declaration about having the upper hand - \\"You're my plaything.\\" While there's some concession that he's worked to make himself appealing/available --  \\"I've ransacked myself, I've flatpacked for your ease\\" -- there should be no mistaking he's in control, and the song ends with \\"I concur, I conquer.\\"<br /><br />6. Invisible - 2:58 - Tom takes the lead vocal here with a song about the rest of the world becoming irrelevant when a couple is in the throes of love: \\"Things vanish one by one, invisible watch them go.\\"<br /><br />7. Albatross - 3:12 - Beautiful slow-tempo song featuring Hayden on vocals. Albatross is a play on the expression of an \\"albatross around my neck.\\" In this case, the unwanted burden is a lover whom he blames for \\"all of these things I've been through.\\" Still he realizes, with the lover hanging around his neck, that they're in their fate together. Amid all the synthesized sounds there's some beautiful piano playing.<br /><br />8. Reach a Bit Further - 3:36 - In a gorgeous, mid tempo song, Hayden & Tom take turns singing lead vocal. The lyrics are about apologizing to a lover for bad behavior (\\"I was angry and brash as a bull\\"). But with Hayden's lines there's a mischievous bent, as if he's looking for credit for his obsequious stance (\\"Will you by any chance, remember the olive branch?\\"). With Tom's lines, I'm not clear if he's seconding the apology to that third -party lover or if they're intended as a response to Hayden's request - as Tom sings, \\"Yes I will do all the things you ask of me,\\" and it becomes a back and forth dialogue between two lovers. However you read it, the song's a beautiful illustration of how interesting their two voices are juxtaposed.<br /><br />9. Burning - 4:44 - Starts with a jangly synthesized sound and then Tom sings about a lover saving him. He's grateful for the passion his lover has brought him - it feels like he's saved him from a burning building. But while he admires his lover's boldness and courage, he knows he can't match it. (\\"I see you walk into the room, do the thing I can't do\\"). He has to admit that by comparison, he's tentative and weak as different verses end with: \\"I'm ashamed,\\" \\"I'm afraid,\\" and finally, \\"I'll wait.\\"<br /><br />10. End Come Too Soon - 7:33 -The song begins with one of those slightly repetitive rhythms that circles around itself but keeps getting wider and stronger with each revolution. (I saw Here We Go Magic open for The Walkmen and they do a lot of this.) When the lyric portion begins, Hayden sings about disappointment over the fact that the act of physical passion can't last forever, and always must have an end. He sings the praises of his lover: \\"Whose butter fingers read me like Braille? Whose dirty mouth would have made Mary hail? Whose wholesome heart had bore down on me? Who ushers dreamers into such harems?\\" Then those circling rhythms come back in a mesmerizing way, with some chant-like shouting, before Hayden returns to complain, \\"It's too soon\\" and \\"The end it comes too soon.\\"ant when a couple is in the throes of love: \\"Things vanish one by one, invisible watch them go.\\"    7. B004LL1HM4|5.0|This album has all the same elements that made Fleet Foxes' first, self-titled album spectacular - Robin Pecknold's wonderful, high voice, the terrific harmonizing of the rest of the group, and the beautiful playing of these classic folk/Appalachian instruments - guitar, mandolin, fiddle and more. Thematically, the album is positively brilliant. The songs really carry the listener through an emotional cycle, beginning with regret over self-absorption, to songs about both pain and anger over lost loved ones (not just lovers, but perhaps a father figure too in \\"Sim Sala Bim\\"). The ambivalence and pain over difficult relationships then gives way, ironically in one of the most musically upbeat songs (\\"Blue Spotted Tail\\") to despair that life may have no meaning at all. But that nadir of hope relinquishes to a final, stirring wish in the close-out song, \\"Grown Ocean,\\" that there may be something more, something bigger behind everything, even though the singer is resigned to the fact that he may have to \\"wait all my life to attain it.\\"<br /><br />The 12 songs on the album are:<br />1. Montezuma - 3:37 - A beautiful slow tempo-ed lament about having too much ego. Now that he's reached the age his parent were when they were children, Robin rues the self-centeredness of his youth (\\"could I wash my hands of just looking out for me\\"). As he realizes everyone meets the same end (\\"the slave and the empress will return to the dirt\\"), he's embarrassed by his former self, \\"Oh man what I used to be.\\" (Some astute observers on songmeanings.net noted the line about \\"Montezuma to Tripoli\\" is an allusion to the Marine fight song, which I take as a reference to his former gung-ho-ness.)<br /><br />2. Bedouin Dress - 4:30 - Opens with sweet guitar picking and a foot-stomping rhythm. The singer expresses more regret over the selfish one-sidedness of his past relationships (\\"If to borrow is to take and not return I have borrowed all my lonesome life.\\") He then hopes he can get to Innisfree - the island that served as a metaphor in the famous Yeats poem for the idealistic escape. The song concludes with a memory of seeing a lost lover in a dress so beautiful its design still sticks in his mind. There's some great jazzy sax playing that will be echoed in a later song that also has an Innisfree reference.<br /><br />3. Sim Sala Bim - 3:14 - A wonderful slow, then fast-tempoed song about abandonment by someone important - a father figure maybe. The singer has no respect for what that person's doing now (\\"are you off somewhere reciting incantations/Sim Sala Bim on your tongue\\"), but the singer had his revenge (\\"Remember when you had me cut off your hair, Call me Delilah then I wouldn't care\\"). The final minute is an instrumental, lyric-less flourish of great, fast-tempoed guitar picking with an electric violin and mandolin accompaniment.<br /><br />4. Battery Kinzie - 2:49 - A beautiful mid-tempo song in which Robin sings about losing a woman to another man, who keeps her under tight control.<br /><br />5. The Plains/Bitter Dancer - 5:54 - This song opens with one of those beautiful crescendo-ing chants that was featured more prominently in the last album. When Robin's lyric comes in, it expresses a concern over the return of a troublesome figure (\\"At arm's length I will hold you there\\"). Then there's a short, beautiful a capella bridge in the middle of the song, which gives way to more of that gorgeous harmonizing, simply with repetitions of \\"Oh, oh.\\" Terrific flute playing is laced throughout.<br /><br />6. Helpessness Blues - 5:03 - Begins with another regret about having been raised by the kind of parenting style that gives a child an exaggerated sense of his own importance. Now he longs to be a small part of something larger. (\\"I'd rather be a functioning cog in some great machinery serving something beyond me.\\") That gives way to a fantasy of a simple rural life with a lover. (\\"If I had an orchard, I'd work till I'm sore and you would wait tables andsoon run the store.\\") But then, in what seems a subtle recognition of the fact that the band's folk/Appalachian style of music probably fosters an illusion about their leading a simple, rural lifestyle, he admits he'd like to live up to the fabrication (\\"Someday I'll be the like the man on the screen\\").<br /><br />7. The Cascades - 2:04 - A short, but wonderful instrumental with great guitar, mandolin playing.<br /><br />8. Lorelai - 4:25 - Very upbeat guitar-picking and chanting harmonies, lead into a lyric about annoyance - sung with a beautiful lilt - over becoming irrelevant to an ex-lover. (\\"So I guess I got old, I was like trash on the sidewalk\\" and the catchiest chorus line on the album, \\"I was old news, old news to you then.\\")<br /><br />9. Someone You'd Admire - 2:29 - A slow tempoed song about the multiple personalities - good & evil - that lurk within us, clawing to get out. (\\"One of them wants only to be someone you'd admire; One would as soon just throw you on the fire.\\") Great concluding line about not knowing which side will win out: \\"God only knows which one of them I'll become.\\"<br /><br />10. The Shrine/An Argument - 8:07 -- This gorgeous-sounding and masterful, mini-epic of a song has four distinct parts. In the mid-tempo opening, Robin's sings about times and people gone by and his sense of loss about them, even though he has \\"sunlight over me no matter I do.\\" He puts a penny in a wishing fountain, clearly hoping for reunion with a lost love. Then the tempo picks up and there's an almost angry remembrance of his lover leaving him and erasing him from her memory. (\\"In the ocean washing my name from your throat.\\"  In the 3rd section he's back totrying to talk himself into feeling hopeful in his solitude (\\"Green apples hang from my tree, they belong only to me\\"), but wishing the ocean will carry him out, again, to that metaphorical island of escape - Innisfree. The fourth, and concluding, section is an instrumental with some very modern jazzy saxphone, and the agitation in those sax blurts doesn't suggest he'll find the escape he's hoping for. It's an echo of the sax played with the earlier reference to the Yeats' poem (in \\"Bedouin Dress\\") but the playing now possesses a tension not present in the earlier, simpler fantasy.<br /><br />11. Blue Spotted Tail - 3:05 - Tonally - with the style of singing and playing - this is the easiest going, most upbeat song on the album. But the lyrics themselves are the bleakest. All the existential themes explored in the other songs have now bottomed out to despair and defeat. (\\"Why is life made only for to end?\\" and \\"Why is the earth moving round the sun? Floating in the vacuum with no purpose, not a one.\\")<br /><br />12. Grown Ocean - 4:36 - An ubeat, rocking conclusion about a vision of one day attaining a state of wisdom (Heaven, Nirvana, earthly happiness .. whatever), and there's a hope - which the rousing music makes you feel could be possible - that a state can be attained where everything will make so much sense, you won't need anyone to come to you with answers anymore. The song & album concludes with a beautiful a cappella set of lines about wanting this to dream to come true.tle recognition of the fact that the band's folk/Appalachian style of music probably fosters an illusion about their leading a simple, rural lifestyle, he admits he'd like to live up to the fabrication (\\"Someday I'll be the like the man on the screen\\").    7.
